06:43:43","updated":"2022-01-06 21:16:11","type":"live_video_scheduled","caption":"7th Annual Celebration - American Indian Heritage Day in Texas","description":"AMERICAN INDIAN HERITAGE DAY IN TEXAS (AIHD) honors the history, culture, education and social contributions made by American Indians communities and leaders.  The vision is to promote understanding in the values of,  respect for American Indians,  and to celebrate the American Indian heritages and cultures.  In order to do this the AIHD introduced a bill to the Texas legislation to create a special state holiday, especially to educate the public on the American Indian tribes. In May 2013, the governor of Texas signed H.R. Bill 174 that designated the last Friday in September as American Indian Heritage Day in Texas. 11:43:58","updated":"2022-01-06 20:14:37","type":"photo","message":"July is Minority Mental Health Month. For our two-part series, we asked Dr. Beena Parikh to give us her perspective on Minority Mental Health month, especially as it relates to the South Asian community. Beena M. Parikh, PhD is a Central Florida native and University of South Florida alumni, she obtained her PhD in Clinical Psychology from Nova Southeastern University in Ft. Lauderdale. Beena Parikh is a charter member of the Indian American Chamber of Commerce, Beena was instrumental to the success of IACC's inaugural India Day with the Orlando Magic: an exciting showcase of Indian culture, cuisine and entertainment. Why do you think South Asians suffer in silence when it comes to mental health issues? In most Eastern cultures, conversations regarding emotions are generally uncomfortable. Most people are not taught how to engage in those conversations, and they are definitely not modeled for them. It becomes an unspoken rule that \"we don't talk about things that bother us\". Particularly in South Asia, where there is an emphasis on spirituality and wholistic living, the notion that a person is \"struggling\" is often met with advice and suggestions around eating better, meditating, doing yoga, or going to the temple more. These strategies, while they have their place, without the opportunity to express their struggles and give it a voice, they often do little to actually alleviate them. In fact, the issues will more often increase until they are addressed directly. What resources are available to our community to address stress, anxiety and depression? This is a problem not just for our South Asian community, but the overall community. Unfortunately, there are not enough professional resources available. Having said that, there are tons of support groups and places that an individual can seek out to help them manage their struggles. Within most churches, schools and workplace organizations there are referral networks available. Also, our improving world of technology has given us the ability to provide tele-therapy opportunities. Michael Phelps tossed his support to one such option that recently hit the air waves. Lastly, a good old fashioned google search for a psychologist or therapist will result in names and details of the therapist and what their strengths, approaches, and strategies are. #IACCOrlando I knew I wanted to write something bold and loud. The “good girl” is always glorified in our society. The girl who is sweet, noncontroversial, the good daughter, the good mother, the good wife – these are things society expects us as women to emulate. Unfortunately, along with these labels come other more limiting expectations. To be humble you can’t be confident, to be noncontroversial you can’t speak your mind, to be respectful you can’t challenge archaic traditions, to be a good mother you have to put everyone else first, to be sweet you can’t be sexy. I’ve always found these expectations extremely suffocating. We as women and people in general have so many different facets to ourselves. Having one quality doesn’t negate the other. If being confident, self-aware, sexy or outspoken are offensive and go against what some think a “good” girl should be, then call me bad. I have no interest in fulfilling someone else’s fantasy of the good girl, I’m too busy fulfilling my own dreams. “Call Me Baddie” is a rejection of the idea that there is a perfect woman. The song is an ode to the rebellious and unapologetic side we all have. It’s a bold self-love anthem encouraging everyone, both men and women, to embrace themselves and shrug off society’s expectation of who they should be.\" - Movina Nagarajan Movina Nagarajan released her first official single “Call Me Baddie” a couple months ago. It is pop song with Indian and hip hop musical influences. She wrote the song for all baddies out there who are following their dreams, putting in the hard work, and doing it all with style. 11:51:55","updated":"2022-01-06 20:14:37","type":"photo","message":"The last part of our series with Dr. Beena Parikh. We are discussing Minority Mental Health Month for July, focusing on the South Asian community. Do you believe that mental health factors, such as stress, anxiety and depression affect our physical health? Without question our mental health also affects our physical health. Without going into a full anatomy lesson, the most primitive part of our brain is responsible for triggering our ability to fight or flee from \"danger\". It cannot think for us, it simply reacts to signals from either the environment outside, or from within, that there is danger. In our society today, danger is more often stress, rather than actual life-threatening danger. This part of the brain tells our muscles to get ready, tells our eyes to widen, our heart rate and breathing to increase so that we can have more oxygen to our brain. Until that part of the brain receives the \"all clear\" message, it's going to continue preparing you to handle danger. When our bodies and brains are exposed to that level of stress responses for extended periods of time, we wear down and eventually break down. Eventually in the absence of a real danger or ultimately an \"all clear signal\", the physical impact can be debilitating. Why is therapy considered taboo in the South Asian culture, and what can we do as a society to change that stigma? The words that someone may have mental health problems raises more anxiety than anxiety itself. Our South Asian cultures are often afraid to open up and admit that they may be dealing with problems within them or their families. As a staunchly religious culture and one that believes so strongly in the notions of karma and dharma (duty), there is often a sense that somehow the family/person was to blame and has somehow \"brought\" it on themselves (perhaps they are not vegetarians, perhaps they don't frequent a temple, or perhaps they or their loved ones are not engaging in good behaviors.) People don't want to associate with those types people and their problems for fear that the same fate may perhaps be awaiting them. The only way to change this stigma is to talk as often, and as openly as possible. As people's confidence and belief in others that they will not be judged increases, so will their ability to open up about their struggles. As a psychologist, what advice would you give to individuals who suffer in silence? Don't do it. Talk to someone, anyone. Perhaps a stranger is exactly what you need to not feel the stigma. Perhaps a close friend or family member has actually gone through much of the same and can provide support. No matter who it may be, find someone and talk to them. Don't stay quiet. It's not just in your head. #IACCOrlando #MinorityHealth #July Singh and Mr. Asim Mandal. The event was coordinated by Mr. SathishNanjappa, the secretary of India Association of St. Louis. Ashwin Patel, one of the community leaders and the owner of Seema Word Travel of St. Louis helped with not only Notary services, but also details regarding other amenities available to the people of Indian origin, such as OCI, Passport renewals, lost passports etc. The effort was extremely successful due to the sponsorship and co-operation of the Directors of the two earliest Indian organizations that were incorporated in St. Louis. The event started promptly at 10am and ended at 3:30 pm as publicized. Over 50 families availed the opportunity. Many were disappointed and left as the services did not include the issuance of the visas, acceptance of OCI applications and renewal of passports on the spot. However, other services that included attestations of important documents was well received. Mr. Krishna Rao, the immediate past President and HaridasKartha the President elect of India Association coordinated the activities on the floor. Mr. Sharath Rao, President of the Hindu temple took the group around the newly built facilities of the Hindu temple and the temple itself. As there was no time for the officers to go site seeing, their tour was limited to the premises only, although volunteers were available for a city tour. Although the officers had an initial apprehension about their visit, the cordiality of the Directors of these organization made a lasting impression. In fact they said they would hope to visit St. Louis as often as possible.
